## Transcript

- [00:00] Question: Why do people do horrible things?
- [00:03] Slave owners, and Nazis, any of the perpetrators of history's atrocities. How do they so successfully
- [00:10] dehumanize other people for so long? At a smaller scale, how do bullies in the lunchroom
- [00:15] manage to treat other kids with such cruelty and then go home and pet their dog and call
- [00:19] their grandma and say "happy birthday?"
- [00:21] Most of what we've been studying so far has focused on the individual. We've covered sub-fields
- [00:25] of psychology like cognitive, personality, and clinical psychology, which tend to address
- [00:29] the phenomena contained within a single person's mind. But there's also social psychology,
- [00:34] which focuses on the power of the situation. It examines how we think about, influence,
- [00:38] and relate to one another in certain conditions. And it's better equipped to answer this question
- [00:43] about people doing horrible things.
- [00:45] Social psychology can not only give us some of the tools we need to understand why people
- [00:48] behave brutally, it can also help us understand why we sometimes act heroically.
- [00:52] Like why did Jean Valjean reveal his true identity to save some stranger from being
- [00:56] tried in his place? And why did Nazi Oskar Schindler risk his own hide to save over a
- [01:01] thousand Jewish people? What made Darth Vader throw the Emperor down that hole, even as
- [01:06] he was being electrocuted?
- [01:08] I can't say there are any easy answers about humanity's greatness or it's horribleness.
- [01:12] Certainly, there aren't any that we can find in the next ten minutes. But we can point
- [01:16] ourselves in the right direction, and it starts with social thinking.
- [01:30] When we're trying to understand why people act like villains or heroes, one of the things
- [01:33] we're really asking is, "Did they do what they did because of their personality? Or
- [01:37] their situation?" Austrian psychologist Fritz Heider began plumbing the depths of this question
- [01:41] in the 1920s when he was developing what's now known as the Attribution Theory.
- [01:45] This theory simply suggests that we can explain someone's behavior by crediting either their
- [01:50] stable, enduring traits - also known as their disposition - or the situation at hand. And
- [01:55] we tend to attribute people's behavior to either one or the other. Sounds pretty simple,
- [01:59] but it can be surprisingly hard to tell whether someone's behavior is dispositional or situational.
- [02:05] Say you see Bruno at a party and he's acting like a wallflower all night. You might assume
- [02:09] that he just has a shy personality. But maybe he doesn't; maybe he'd ordinarily be re-enacting
- [02:13] all the moves from Footloose at this party but on this night, he had a twisted ankle
- [02:17] or a headache or he'd just seen his ex with somebody new - those are all situational explanations.
- [02:23] Overestimating the forces of personality while underestimating the power of the situation
- [02:28] is called the Fundamental Attribution Error. And as you can imagine, making this kind of
- [02:32] error can really end up warping your opinion of another person and lead to false snap judgments.
- [02:38] This might not be such a big deal when it comes to Bruno and his awesome dance moves
- [02:42] but according to one study of college students, 7 in 10 women report that men have misread
- [02:47] their polite friendliness - which would be appropriate for the situation - as a sexual come-on.
- [02:52] We choose how we explain other people's behavior everyday and what we choose to believe can
- [02:57] have big consequences. For example, our political views will likely be strongly influenced by
- [03:01] whether we decide to attribute poverty or homelessness to personal dispositions, like
- [03:05] being lazy and looking for a hand-out, or social circumstances like lack of education and opportunity.
- [03:10] And these attitudes can, in turn, affect our actions. Activists and politicians know this
- [03:14] well and they can use it to their advantage to persuade people in different ways.
- [03:18] In the late 1970s and 80s, psychologist Richard Petty and John Cacioppo developed a dual process
- [03:24] theory of understanding how persuasion works. The first part of their model is known as
- [03:28] the Central Route Persuasion and it involves calling on basic thinking and reasoning to convince people.
- [03:33] This is what's at work when interested people focus on the evidence and arguments at hand,
- [03:37] and are persuaded by the actual content of the message. So when you're watching a political
- [03:41] debate, you might be persuaded by a candidate's particular policies, positions or voting history.
- [03:45] That is, the stuff they're actually sayin'.
- [03:47] But we all know that persuasion involves more than that. There is also Peripheral Route
- [03:52] Persuasion at work. This influences people by the way of incidental cues, like a speaker's
- [03:56] physical attractiveness or personal relatability.
- [03:59] There's not a lot of hard thinking going on here, it's more of a gut reaction. So you might decide
- [04:03] to vote for a particular candidate because you think they're cute or they're from your home town.
- [04:06] Peripheral Route Persuasion happens more readily when you're not paying a ton of attention,
- [04:11] which is why billboards and television ads can be scarily effective. So that's how politicians
- [04:15] and advertisers and maybe bosses and teachers and pushy friends try to change our behavior
- [04:20] by changing our attitudes.
- [04:22] But, it turns out that the reverse is true too. Our attitudes can be affected by our
- [04:27] behaviors. You might have heard about the phrase, "Fake it till you make it." Meaning,
- [04:29] if you smile when you're actually sad the act of smiling may carry you through an attitude
- [04:34] change until you actually feel better.
- [04:36] Sometimes we can manipulate ourselves this way, but it's also an incredibly effective
- [04:39] method people use to persuade each other. It generally works best in increments, through
- [04:44] what psychologists call the foot-in-the-door phenomenon. People tend to more readily comply
- [04:48] with a big request after they've first agreed to smaller more innocuous requests.
- [04:52] Like Darth Vader didn't just go from "Go get 'em Anakin," to Dark Lord overnight. He was
- [04:56] slowly enticed to the dark side, by a series of escalating actions and attitude changes.
- [05:02] Do this favor for me, now run this errand, now kill these Padawans. Now blow up a planet!
- [05:07] What started this small actions went on to become big ones, suddenly transforming Vader's
- [05:11] belief's about himself and others.
- [05:13] There's plenty of experimental evidence that moral action really does strengthens moral
- [05:18] convictions, just as amoral action strengthen amoral attitudes. And there is perhaps no
- [05:23] better example of this than the Stanford Prison Experiment.
- [05:26] Back in 1971 Stanford psych professor Philip Zimbardo and his team put an ad in the local
- [05:31] paper looking for volunteers to participate in a 14 day experiment. After screening around
- [05:36] 70 applicants, 24 male college students were deemed physically and mentally fit enough
- [05:41] to participate in the study. For their troubles they'd each be given $15 a day.
- [05:45] The participants didn't know the exact nature of the experiment, just that it involved a
- [05:49] fake prison situation. And with a coin flip, half were randomly deemed prisoners and the
- [05:54] other half guards. The guards were told that it was the prisoner's behavior that was being
- [05:58] studied. The prisoners weren't told much of anything, aside from that they had been arrested
- [06:02] and taken to prison. Other than that neither group had many specific instructions.
- [06:07] Zimbardo wanted to observe how each party adapted to their roles, and so, on a quiet
- [06:12] Sunday summer morning in Palo Alto, real cops swooped in and arrested the prisoners in their
- [06:18] homes under charges of robbery. They were frisked, handcuffed, and read their rights.
- [06:23] Back at the station, they were formally booked and then blindfolded in a holding cell wearing
- [06:27] only hospital gowns. The researchers had taken great care to make sure that the setting was
- [06:31] extremely realistic, which is one reason they used real cops in the arrest before handing
- [06:36] the prisoners over to the fake guards. And it took no time at all for this role-playing
- [06:41] to become really, really real.
- [06:43] The initial trauma of the humiliation of the arrest, the booking, strip-searching and waiting,
- [06:48] immediately kicked off a loss of identity in the prisoners. A few prisoners only made
- [06:52] it through the first night before they became too emotionally distressed and had to be released.
- [06:57] Things only went downhill from there. Though the guards could act any way they wanted as
- [07:01] long as they didn't physically hurt anyone, encounters quickly became cruel, hostile,
- [07:05] and dehumanizing. Guards hurled insults and commands, referred to the prisoners only by
- [07:09] number, and put some of them in solitary confinement. Prisoners started breaking down, others rebelled,
- [07:14] and still others became passively resigned as if they deserved to be treated so badly.
- [07:19] Things got bad enough that the experiment ended after only six days, causing relief
- [07:23] in the fake prisoners, while interestingly leaving some fake guards feeling angry.
- [07:28] Luckily, everyone involved bounced back to normal once out of the prison setting. All
- [07:32] of those negative moods and abusive behaviors were situational, and that fact reinforced
- [07:37] the important concept that the power of a given situation can easily override individual
- [07:42] differences in personality. Although it would never fly by today's ethical standards, Zimbardo's
- [07:46] famous study remains influential today because it sheds such a harsh light on the nature
- [07:50] of power and corruption.
- [07:52] And yet, people differ. Many people succumb and become compliant in terrible situations,
- [07:56] but not everyone does. Lots of people risked their lives to hide Jewish people in World
- [08:00] War II, help runaway slaves along the Underground Railroad, keep Tutsi refugees safe during
- [08:04] the Rwandan genocide, or generally refuse to comply or participate in actions they didn't
- [08:09] believe in. Some people can, and do resist turning to the dark side, even when it seems
- [08:13] like everyone around them is going mad. And yet,  the fact is, these people tend to be in the minority.
- [08:19] So why? Why does it seem so easy to rationalize a negative action or attitude and so hard
- [08:24] to muster the positive ones? One partial explanation comes from American social psychologist Leon
- [08:29] Festinger's theory of cognitive dissonance. It's one of the most important concepts in psychology.
- [08:34] Festinger's theory begins with the notion that we experience discomfort - or dissonance
- [08:38] - when our thoughts, beliefs, or behaviors are inconsistent with each other.
- [08:42] Basically, we don't like to confuse ourselves.
- [08:44] For example, if Bruno was generally considered a peaceful person but finds himself suddenly
- [08:48] punching at his friend over a fender-bender, he's likely experiencing some level of cognitive dissonance.
- [08:53] So, by Festinger's thinking, Bruno might relieve this tension by actually modifying his beliefs
- [08:58] in order to match the action's he's already committed, like telling himself, "Turns out,
- [09:02] I'm not such a nice guy after all, maybe I'm actually a bully." On the other hand, he might
- [09:06] resolve his internal tension by changing how he thinks about the situation. He might still
- [09:10] think of himself as a peaceful person, but realize that an unusual situation led to an
- [09:15] unusual action, like, he'd had a bad day and it was his mom's new car, or his friend was
- [09:20] just really askin' for it. So, he can keep being the ordinarily peaceful guy he was before.
- [09:25] It's kind of an inverted fundamental attribution error if you think about it. Attributing a
- [09:29] person's actions mainly to the situation, instead of his personality. The point is that
- [09:33] this mismatch between what we do and who we think we are induces tension - cognitive dissonance
- [09:38] - and that we tend to want to resolve that tension. That's part of what turns an Anakin
- [09:42] into a Darth Vader, and then, if we're lucky, back into an Anakin.
- [09:46] Today you learned that social psychology studies how people relate to each other. We discussed
- [09:50] Fritz Heider's attribution theory, and fundamental attribution error. You also learned how attitudes
- [09:56] can affect actions, like through the duel-process theory of persuasion, and also how behavior
- [10:01] can change attitudes, like through the foot-in-the-door phenomenon. The Stanford prison experiment
- [10:06] illustrated how a situation can override individual differences in personality, while Leon Festinger's
- [10:11] theory of cognitive dissonance explained how we ease the tension between conflicting thoughts and actions.
